				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about the Sun and its planetary system. <td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top:1px solid #aaa;font-size:90%;text-align:left;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa; padding:0.4em 0 0.5em 4.8em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_system.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Solar system.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/13px-Solar_system.jpg" width="13" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/19px-Solar_system.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Solar_system.jpg/26px-Solar_system.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="5600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Solar_System" title="Portal:Solar System">Solar System portal</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/File:He1523a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="He1523a.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/He1523a.jpg/14px-He1523a.jpg" width="14" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/He1523a.jpg/21px-He1523a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/He1523a.jpg/28px-He1523a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="207" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Star" title="Portal:Star">Star portal</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Solar_System_sidebar" title="Template:Solar System sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Solar_System_sidebar" title="Template talk:Solar System sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Solar_System_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Solar System</b><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[a]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitationally</a> bound system comprising the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the objects that orbit it, either directly or indirectly.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[b]</a></sup> Of those objects that orbit the Sun directly, the largest eight are the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[c]</a></sup> with the remainder being significantly smaller objects, such as <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a>. Of the objects that orbit the Sun indirectly, the <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, two are larger than the smallest planet, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[d]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Solar System <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formed 4.6 billion years ago</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of a giant interstellar <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a>. The vast majority of the system's <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> is in the Sun, with most of the remaining mass contained in <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. The four smaller inner planets, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>, being primarily composed of rock and metal. The four outer planets are <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>, being substantially more massive than the terrestrials. The two largest, Jupiter and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>, being composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>; the two outermost planets, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>, being composed mostly of substances with relatively high melting points compared with hydrogen and helium, called <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a>, such as water, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. All planets have almost circular orbits that lie within a nearly flat disc called the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>.</p>
<p>The Solar System also contains smaller objects.<sup id="cite_ref-footnoteB_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-14">[e]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, which lies between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter, mostly contains objects composed, like the terrestrial planets, of rock and metal. Beyond Neptune's orbit lie the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>, which are populations of <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian objects</a> composed mostly of ices, and beyond them a newly discovered population of <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">sednoids</a>. Within these populations are several dozen to possibly tens of thousands of objects large enough that they have been rounded by their own gravity.<sup id="cite_ref-Stern2012_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2012-15">[10]</a></sup> Such objects are categorized as <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>. Identified dwarf planets include the asteroid <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> and the trans-Neptunian objects <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-footnoteB_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-14">[e]</a></sup> In addition to these two regions, various other small-body populations, including <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">centaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">interplanetary dust clouds</a>, freely travel between regions. Six of the planets, at least four of the dwarf planets, and many of the smaller bodies are orbited by <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[f]</a></sup> usually termed "moons" after the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Each of the outer planets is encircled by <a href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetary ring">planetary rings</a> of dust and other small objects.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, a stream of charged particles flowing outwards from the Sun, creates a bubble-like region in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">heliopause</a> is the point at which pressure from the solar wind is equal to the opposing pressure of the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>; it extends out to the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>, which is thought to be the source for <a href="/wiki/Long-period_comet" class="mw-redirect" title="Long-period comet">long-period comets</a>, may also exist at a distance roughly a thousand times further than the heliosphere. The Solar System is located in the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a>, 26,000 light-years from the center of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_exploration">Discovery and exploration</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discovery_and_exploration_of_the_Solar_System" title="Discovery and exploration of the Solar System">Discovery and exploration of the Solar System</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heliocentric.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/220px-Heliocentric.jpg" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/330px-Heliocentric.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Heliocentric.jpg/440px-Heliocentric.jpg 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heliocentric.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Andreas_Cellarius" title="Andreas Cellarius">Andreas Cellarius</a>'s illustration of the Copernican system, from the <i>Harmonia Macrocosmica</i> (1660)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For most of history, humanity did not recognize or understand the concept of the Solar System. Most people up to the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>–<a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> believed Earth to be stationary at the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> and categorically different from the divine or ethereal objects that moved through the sky. Although the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> had speculated on a heliocentric reordering of the cosmos, <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> was the first to develop a mathematically predictive <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[12]</a></sup> In the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> developed an understanding of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> that led to the gradual acceptance of the idea that Earth moves around the Sun and that the planets are governed by the same physical laws that governed Earth. The invention of the telescope led to the discovery of further planets and moons. Improvements in the telescope and the use of <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft">unmanned spacecraft</a> have enabled the investigation of geological phenomena, such as <a href="/wiki/Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountains">mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">craters</a>, seasonal meteorological phenomena, such as <a href="/wiki/Clouds" class="mw-redirect" title="Clouds">clouds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dust_storms" class="mw-redirect" title="Dust storms">dust storms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_caps" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice caps">ice caps</a> on the other planets.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_composition">Structure and composition</span></h2>
<p>The principal component of the Solar System is the Sun, a <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G2 main-sequence star</a> that contains 99.86% of the system's known mass and dominates it gravitationally.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[13]</a></sup> The Sun's four largest orbiting bodies, the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>, account for 99% of the remaining mass, with Jupiter and Saturn together comprising more than 90%. The remaining objects of the Solar System (including the four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_moons" class="mw-redirect" title="List of moons">moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>) together comprise less than 0.002% of the Solar System's total mass.<sup id="cite_ref-footnoteD_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteD-23">[g]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most large objects in orbit around the Sun lie near the plane of Earth's orbit, known as the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. The planets are very close to the ecliptic, whereas comets and <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> objects are frequently at significantly greater angles to it.<sup id="cite_ref-Levison2003_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levison2003-24">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[18]</a></sup> All the planets, and most other objects, orbit the Sun in the same direction that the Sun is rotating (counter-clockwise, as viewed from above Earth's north pole).<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[19]</a></sup> There are <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Retrograde motion">exceptions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">Halley's Comet</a>.</p>
<p>The overall structure of the charted regions of the Solar System consists of the Sun, four relatively small inner planets surrounded by a belt of mostly rocky asteroids, and four giant planets surrounded by the Kuiper belt of mostly icy objects. Astronomers sometimes informally divide this structure into separate regions. The inner Solar System includes the four terrestrial planets and the asteroid belt. The outer Solar System is beyond the asteroids, including the four giant planets.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[20]</a></sup> Since the discovery of the Kuiper belt, the outermost parts of the Solar System are considered a distinct region consisting of the objects beyond Neptune.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Size_planets_comparison.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Size_planets_comparison.jpg/280px-Size_planets_comparison.jpg" width="280" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Size_planets_comparison.jpg/420px-Size_planets_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Size_planets_comparison.jpg/560px-Size_planets_comparison.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1181" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Size_planets_comparison.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The eight planets of the Solar System (by decreasing size) are <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of the planets in the Solar System have secondary systems of their own, being orbited by planetary objects called <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>, or moons (two of which, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, are larger than the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>), and, in the case of the four giant planets, by <a href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetary ring">planetary rings</a>, thin bands of tiny particles that orbit them in unison. Most of the largest natural satellites are in <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" class="mw-redirect" title="Synchronous rotation">synchronous rotation</a>, with one face permanently turned toward their parent.</p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:285px;max-width:285px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px">

<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif/137px-Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif" width="137" height="139" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif/206px-Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif 2x" data-file-width="248" data-file-height="251" /></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">All planets of the Solar System lie very close to the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. The closer they are to the Sun, the faster they travel <i>(<a href="/wiki/Inner_planets" class="mw-redirect" title="Inner planets">inner planets</a> on the left, all planets except Neptune on the right)</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws of planetary motion</a> describe the orbits of objects about the Sun. Following Kepler's laws, each object travels along an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> with the Sun at one <a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">focus</a>. Objects closer to the Sun (with smaller <a href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a>) travel more quickly because they are more affected by the Sun's gravity. On an elliptical orbit, a body's distance from the Sun varies over the course of its year. A body's closest approach to the Sun is called its <i><a href="/wiki/Perihelion" class="mw-redirect" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a></i>, whereas its most distant point from the Sun is called its <i><a href="/wiki/Aphelion" class="mw-redirect" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a></i>. The orbits of the planets are nearly circular, but many comets, asteroids, and Kuiper belt objects follow highly elliptical orbits. The positions of the bodies in the Solar System can be predicted using <a href="/wiki/Numerical_model_of_the_Solar_System" title="Numerical model of the Solar System">numerical models</a>.</p>
<p>Although the Sun dominates the system by mass, it accounts for only about 2% of the <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marochnik1995_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marochnik1995-29">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[23]</a></sup> The planets, dominated by Jupiter, account for most of the rest of the angular momentum due to the combination of their mass, orbit, and distance from the Sun, with a possibly significant contribution from comets.<sup id="cite_ref-Marochnik1995_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marochnik1995-29">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sun, which comprises nearly all the matter in the Solar System, is composed of roughly 98% hydrogen and helium.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, which comprise nearly all the remaining matter, are also primarily composed of hydrogen and helium.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[26]</a></sup> A composition gradient exists in the Solar System, created by heat and <a href="/wiki/Light_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Light pressure">light pressure</a> from the Sun; those objects closer to the Sun, which are more affected by heat and light pressure, are composed of elements with high melting points. Objects farther from the Sun are composed largely of materials with lower melting points.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[27]</a></sup> The boundary in the Solar System beyond which those volatile substances could condense is known as the <a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">frost line</a>, and it lies at roughly 5 AU from the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-Mumma_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mumma-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The objects of the inner Solar System are composed mostly of rock,<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-35">[28]</a></sup> the collective name for compounds with high melting points, such as <a href="/wiki/Silicates" class="mw-redirect" title="Silicates">silicates</a>, iron or nickel, that remained solid under almost all conditions in the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_nebula" title="Protoplanetary nebula">protoplanetary nebula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-36">[29]</a></sup> Jupiter and Saturn are composed mainly of gases, the astronomical term for materials with extremely low melting points and high <a href="/wiki/Vapour_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Vapour pressure">vapour pressure</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, which were always in the gaseous phase in the nebula.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-36">[29]</a></sup> Ices, like <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-35">[28]</a></sup> have melting points up to a few hundred kelvins.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-36">[29]</a></sup> They can be found as ices, liquids, or gases in various places in the Solar System, whereas in the nebula they were either in the solid or gaseous phase.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-36">[29]</a></sup> Icy substances comprise the majority of the satellites of the giant planets, as well as most of Uranus and Neptune (the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>") and the numerous small objects that lie beyond Neptune's orbit.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-35">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zeilik_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zeilik-37">[30]</a></sup> Together, gases and ices are referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Placxo_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Placxo-38">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distances_and_scales">Distances and scales</span></h3>
<p>The distance from Earth to the Sun is 1 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a> (150,000,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a>), or AU. For comparison, the radius of the Sun is 0.0047&#160;AU (700,000&#160;km). Thus, the Sun occupies 0.00001% (10<sup>−5</sup>&#160;%) of the volume of a sphere with a radius the size of Earth's orbit, whereas Earth's volume is roughly one millionth (10<sup>−6</sup>) that of the Sun. Jupiter, the largest planet, is 5.2 astronomical units (780,000,000&#160;km) from the Sun and has a radius of 71,000&#160;km (0.00047&#160;AU), whereas the most distant planet, Neptune, is 30&#160;AU (4.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;km) from the Sun.</p>
<p>With a few exceptions, the farther a planet or belt is from the Sun, the larger the distance between its orbit and the orbit of the next nearer object to the Sun. For example, Venus is approximately 0.33 AU farther out from the Sun than Mercury, whereas Saturn is 4.3 AU out from Jupiter, and Neptune lies 10.5 AU out from Uranus. Attempts have been made to determine a relationship between these orbital distances (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Titius%E2%80%93Bode_law" title="Titius–Bode law">Titius–Bode law</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[32]</a></sup> but no such theory has been accepted. The images at the beginning of this section show the orbits of the various constituents of the Solar System on different scales.</p>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Solar_System_model" title="Solar System model">Solar System models</a> attempt to convey the relative scales involved in the Solar System on human terms. Some are small in scale (and may be mechanical—called <a href="/wiki/Orrery" title="Orrery">orreries</a>)—whereas others extend across cities or regional areas.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[33]</a></sup> The largest such scale model, the <a href="/wiki/Sweden_Solar_System" title="Sweden Solar System">Sweden Solar System</a>, uses the 110-metre (361-ft) <a href="/wiki/Ericsson_Globe" title="Ericsson Globe">Ericsson Globe</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> as its substitute Sun, and, following the scale, Jupiter is a 7.5-metre (25-foot) sphere at <a href="/wiki/Arlanda_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Arlanda International Airport">Arlanda International Airport</a>, 40&#160;km (25&#160;mi) away, whereas the farthest current object, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, is a 10-cm (4-in) sphere in <a href="/wiki/Lule%C3%A5" title="Luleå">Luleå</a>, 912&#160;km (567&#160;mi) away.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sedna_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sedna-42">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the Sun–Neptune distance is <a href="/wiki/Solar_System_model" title="Solar System model">scaled</a> to 100 metres, then the Sun would be about 3&#160;cm in diameter (roughly two-thirds the diameter of a golf ball), the giant planets would be all smaller than about 3&#160;mm, and Earth's diameter along with that of the other terrestrial planets would be smaller than a flea (0.3&#160;mm) at this scale.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[36]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:5008px;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Formation and evolution of the Solar System</a></div>
<p>The Solar System formed 4.568 billion years ago from the gravitational collapse of a region within a large <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[h]</a></sup> This initial cloud was likely several light-years across and probably birthed several stars.<sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-45">[37]</a></sup> As is typical of molecular clouds, this one consisted mostly of hydrogen, with some helium, and small amounts of heavier elements fused by previous generations of stars. As the region that would become the Solar System, known as the <a href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar nebula">pre-solar nebula</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[38]</a></sup> collapsed, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a> caused it to rotate faster. The centre, where most of the mass collected, became increasingly hotter than the surrounding disc.<sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-45">[37]</a></sup> As the contracting nebula rotated faster, it began to flatten into a <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Protoplanetary disc">protoplanetary disc</a> with a diameter of roughly 200&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a><sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-45">[37]</a></sup> and a hot, dense <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostar</a> at the centre.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[40]</a></sup> The planets formed by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> from this disc,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[41]</a></sup> in which dust and gas gravitationally attracted each other, coalescing to form ever larger bodies. Hundreds of protoplanets may have existed in the early Solar System, but they either merged or were destroyed, leaving the planets, dwarf planets, and leftover <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">minor bodies</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solarnebula.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Solarnebula.jpg/280px-Solarnebula.jpg" width="280" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Solarnebula.jpg/420px-Solarnebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Solarnebula.jpg 2x" data-file-width="430" data-file-height="318" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Solarnebula.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's concept of the early Solar System</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to their higher boiling points, only metals and silicates could exist in solid form in the warm inner Solar System close to the Sun, and these would eventually form the rocky planets of Mercury, Venus, Earth, and Mars. Because metallic elements only comprised a very small fraction of the solar nebula, the terrestrial planets could not grow very large. The giant planets (Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune) formed further out, beyond the frost line, the point between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter where material is cool enough for volatile icy compounds to remain solid. The ices that formed these planets were more plentiful than the metals and silicates that formed the terrestrial inner planets, allowing them to grow massive enough to capture large atmospheres of hydrogen and helium, the lightest and most abundant elements. Leftover debris that never became planets congregated in regions such as the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a> is an explanation for the creation of these regions and how the outer planets could have formed in different positions and migrated to their current orbits through various gravitational interactions.</p>
<p>Within 50 million years, the pressure and density of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> in the centre of the protostar became great enough for it to begin <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yi2001_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yi2001-50">[42]</a></sup> The temperature, reaction rate, pressure, and density increased until <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a> was achieved: the thermal pressure equalled the force of gravity. At this point, the Sun became a <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> star.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[43]</a></sup> The main-sequence phase, from beginning to end, will last about 10 billion years for the Sun compared to around two billion years for all other phases of the Sun's pre-<a href="/wiki/Stellar_remnant" class="mw-redirect" title="Stellar remnant">remnant</a> life combined.<sup id="cite_ref-mnras386_1_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mnras386_1-52">[44]</a></sup> Solar wind from the Sun created the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a> and swept away the remaining gas and dust from the protoplanetary disc into interstellar space, ending the planetary formation process. The Sun is growing brighter; early in its main-sequence life its brightness was 70% that of what it is today.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Solar System will remain roughly as we know it today until the hydrogen in the core of the Sun has been entirely converted to helium, which will occur roughly 5 billion years from now. This will mark the end of the Sun's main-sequence life. At this time, the core of the Sun will collapse, and the energy output will be much greater than at present. The outer layers of the Sun will expand to roughly 260 times its current diameter, and the Sun will become a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>. Because of its vastly increased surface area, the surface of the Sun will be considerably cooler (2,600 K at its coolest) than it is on the main sequence.<sup id="cite_ref-mnras386_1_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mnras386_1-52">[44]</a></sup> The expanding Sun is expected to vaporize Mercury and render Earth uninhabitable. Eventually, the core will be hot enough for helium fusion; the Sun will burn helium for a fraction of the time it burned hydrogen in the core. The Sun is not massive enough to commence the fusion of heavier elements, and nuclear reactions in the core will dwindle. Its outer layers will move away into space, leaving a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>, an extraordinarily dense object, half the original mass of the Sun but only the size of Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[46]</a></sup> The ejected outer layers will form what is known as a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a>, returning some of the material that formed the Sun—but now enriched with <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">heavier elements</a> like carbon—to the interstellar medium.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sun">Sun</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg/280px-Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg" width="280" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg/420px-Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg/560px-Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1181" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Planets_and_sun_size_comparison.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Size comparison of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Sun is the Solar System's <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> and by far its most massive component. Its large mass (332,900 Earth masses)<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[47]</a></sup> produces temperatures and densities in its <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">core</a> high enough to sustain <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> into <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, making it a <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> star.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[48]</a></sup> This releases an enormous amount of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, mostly <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiated</a> into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a> as <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> peaking in <a href="/wiki/Visible_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Visible light">visible light</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sun is a <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G2-type main-sequence star</a>. Hotter main-sequence stars are more luminous. The Sun's temperature is intermediate between that of the <a href="/wiki/O-type_main-sequence_star" title="O-type main-sequence star">hottest stars</a> and that of the coolest stars. Stars brighter and hotter than the Sun are rare, whereas substantially dimmer and cooler stars, known as <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a>, make up 85% of the stars in the Milky Way.<sup id="cite_ref-sun_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun-58">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sun is a <a href="/wiki/Population_I_stars" class="mw-redirect" title="Population I stars">population I star</a>; it has a higher abundance of elements heavier than hydrogen and helium ("<a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metals</a>" in astronomical parlance) than the older population II stars.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[52]</a></sup> Elements heavier than hydrogen and helium were formed in the cores of ancient and exploding stars, so the first generation of stars had to die before the Universe could be enriched with these atoms. The oldest stars contain few metals, whereas stars born later have more. This high metallicity is thought to have been crucial to the Sun's development of a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary system</a> because the planets form from the accretion of "metals".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interplanetary_medium">Interplanetary medium</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">Interplanetary medium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">Solar wind</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/280px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" width="280" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/420px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/560px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif 2x" data-file-width="866" data-file-height="676" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">heliospheric current sheet</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The vast majority of the Solar System consists of a near-<a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a>. Along with <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">light</a>, the Sun radiates a continuous stream of charged particles (a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>) known as the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>. This stream of particles spreads outwards at roughly 1.5 million kilometres per hour,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[54]</a></sup> creating a tenuous atmosphere that permeates the interplanetary medium out to at least 100&#160;AU <i>(see <a href="#Heliosphere">§&#160;Heliosphere</a>)</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voyager_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-63">[55]</a></sup> Activity on the Sun's surface, such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">coronal mass ejections</a>, disturb the heliosphere, creating <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">space weather</a> and causing <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">geomagnetic storms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SunFlip_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SunFlip-64">[56]</a></sup> The largest structure within the heliosphere is the <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">heliospheric current sheet</a>, a spiral form created by the actions of the Sun's rotating magnetic field on the interplanetary medium.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> stops <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">its atmosphere</a> from being stripped away by the solar wind.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[59]</a></sup> Venus and Mars do not have magnetic fields, and as a result the solar wind is causing their atmospheres to gradually bleed away into space.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">Coronal mass ejections</a> and similar events blow a magnetic field and huge quantities of material from the surface of the Sun. The interaction of this magnetic field and material with Earth's magnetic field funnels charged particles into Earth's upper atmosphere, where its interactions create <a href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurorae</a> seen near the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field#Magnetic_poles" title="Earth's magnetic field">magnetic poles</a>.</p>
<p>The heliosphere and planetary magnetic fields (for those planets that have them) partially shield the Solar System from high-energy interstellar particles called <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>. The density of cosmic rays in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> and the strength of the Sun's magnetic field change on very long timescales, so the level of cosmic-ray penetration in the Solar System varies, though by how much is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-Langner_et_al_2005_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langner_et_al_2005-69">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The interplanetary medium is home to at least two disc-like regions of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a>. The first, the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">zodiacal dust cloud</a>, lies in the inner Solar System and causes the <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">zodiacal light</a>. It was likely formed by collisions within the asteroid belt brought on by gravitational interactions with the planets.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup> The second dust cloud extends from about 10&#160;AU to about 40&#160;AU, and was probably created by similar collisions within the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_Solar_System">Inner Solar System</span></h2>
<p>The <b>inner Solar System</b> is the region comprising the <a href="#Terrestrial_planets">terrestrial planets</a> and the <a href="#Asteroid_belt">asteroid belt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-inner_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inner-73">[65]</a></sup> Composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a> and metals, the objects of the inner Solar System are relatively close to the Sun; the radius of this entire region is less than the distance between the orbits of Jupiter and Saturn. This region is also within the <a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">frost line</a>, which is a little less than 5 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (about 700 million km) from the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_planets"><span id="Terrestrial_planets"></span> Inner planets</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">Terrestrial planet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg/280px-Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg" width="280" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg/420px-Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg/560px-Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1181" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Telluric_planets_size_comparison.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The inner planets. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> (sizes to scale).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The four terrestrial or <b>inner planets</b> have dense, <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky</a> compositions, few or no <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, and no <a href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetary ring">ring systems</a>. They are composed largely of <a href="/wiki/Refractory_(planetary_science)" title="Refractory (planetary science)">refractory</a> minerals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>, which form their <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crusts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantles</a>, and metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, which form their <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">cores</a>. Three of the four inner planets (Venus, Earth and Mars) have <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a> substantial enough to generate <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a>; all have <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonic</a> surface features, such as <a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">rift valleys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>. The term <i>inner planet</i> should not be confused with <i><a href="/wiki/Inferior_planet" class="mw-redirect" title="Inferior planet">inferior planet</a></i>, which designates those planets that are closer to the Sun than Earth is (i.e. Mercury and Venus).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury">Mercury</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury (planet)</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> (0.4&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> from the Sun) is the closest planet to the Sun and the smallest planet in the Solar System (0.055 Earth masses). Mercury has no natural satellites; besides impact craters, its only known geological features are lobed ridges or <a href="/wiki/Rupes" title="Rupes">rupes</a> that were probably produced by a period of contraction early in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[67]</a></sup> Mercury's very tenuous atmosphere consists of atoms blasted off its surface by the solar wind.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[68]</a></sup> Its relatively large iron core and thin mantle have not yet been adequately explained. Hypotheses include that its outer layers were stripped off by a giant impact; or, that it was prevented from fully accreting by the young Sun's energy.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[70]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus">Venus</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> (0.7 AU from the Sun) is close in size to Earth (0.815 Earth masses) and, like Earth, has a thick silicate mantle around an iron core, a substantial atmosphere, and evidence of internal geological activity. It is much drier than Earth, and its atmosphere is ninety times as dense. Venus has no natural satellites. It is the hottest planet, with surface temperatures over 400 <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a> (752°F), most likely due to the amount of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> in the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[71]</a></sup> No definitive evidence of current geological activity has been detected on Venus, but it has no magnetic field that would prevent depletion of its substantial atmosphere, which suggests that its atmosphere is being replenished by volcanic eruptions.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[72]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth">Earth</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> (1 AU from the Sun) is the largest and densest of the inner planets, the only one known to have current geological activity, and the only place where <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> is known to exist.<sup id="cite_ref-life_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-life-81">[73]</a></sup> Its liquid <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a> is unique among the terrestrial planets, and it is the only planet where <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> has been observed. Earth's atmosphere is radically different from those of the other planets, having been altered by the presence of life to contain 21% free <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup> It has one natural satellite, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, the only large satellite of a terrestrial planet in the Solar System.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars">Mars</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> (1.5&#160;AU from the Sun) is smaller than Earth and Venus (0.107 Earth masses). It has an atmosphere of mostly <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> with a surface pressure of 6.1 millibars (roughly 0.6% of that of Earth).<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[75]</a></sup> Its surface, peppered with vast volcanoes, such as <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a>, and rift valleys, such as <a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">Valles Marineris</a>, shows geological activity that may have persisted until as recently as 2 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup> Its red colour comes from <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron oxide</a> (rust) in its soil.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup> Mars has two tiny natural satellites (<a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a>) thought to be captured <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_belt">Asteroid belt</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">Asteroid belt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/220px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/330px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/440px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The donut-shaped <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> is located between the orbits of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>.
<table style="width:100%;">
<tr>
<td valign="top"><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #B3B300; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#FFFF00; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a></span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #36C133; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#6ad768; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojans</a></span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #00508A; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#007DD6; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">Planetary orbit</a></span></td>
<td valign="top"><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #999999; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#e9e9e9; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">Asteroid belt</a></span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #855D00; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#d39300; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> <a href="/wiki/Hilda_family" title="Hilda family">Hilda asteroids</a></span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #940000; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#c90000; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">NEOs</a> <small>(selection)</small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">Asteroids</a> except for the largest, Ceres, are classified as <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a><sup id="cite_ref-footnoteB_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-14">[e]</a></sup> and are composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Refractory_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Refractory (astronomy)">refractory</a> rocky and metallic <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, with some ice.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[80]</a></sup> They range from a few metres to hundreds of kilometres in size. Asteroids smaller than one meter are usually called <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroids" class="mw-redirect" title="Micrometeoroids">micrometeoroids</a> (grain-sized), depending on different, somewhat arbitrary definitions.</p>
<p>The asteroid belt occupies the orbit between Mars and Jupiter, between 2.3 and 3.3&#160;AU from the Sun. It is thought to be remnants from the Solar System's formation that failed to coalesce because of the gravitational interference of Jupiter.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[81]</a></sup> The asteroid belt contains tens of thousands, possibly millions, of objects over one kilometre in diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup> Despite this, the total mass of the asteroid belt is unlikely to be more than a thousandth of that of Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-Krasinsky2002_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krasinsky2002-22">[16]</a></sup> The asteroid belt is very sparsely populated; <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">spacecraft</a> routinely pass through without incident.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceres">Ceres</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres (dwarf planet)</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> (2.77&#160;AU) is the largest asteroid, a <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanet</a>, and a dwarf planet.<sup id="cite_ref-footnoteB_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-14">[e]</a></sup> It has a diameter of slightly under 1,000&#160;km, and a mass large enough for its own gravity to pull it into a spherical shape. Ceres was considered a planet when it was discovered in 1801, and was reclassified to asteroid in the 1850s as further observations revealed additional asteroids.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup> It was classified as a dwarf planet in 2006 when the <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">definition of a planet</a> was created.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_groups">Asteroid groups</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>Asteroids in the asteroid belt are divided into <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Asteroid group">asteroid groups</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_family" title="Asteroid family">families</a> based on their orbital characteristics. <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" class="mw-redirect" title="Asteroid moon">Asteroid moons</a> are asteroids that orbit larger asteroids. They are not as clearly distinguished as planetary moons, sometimes being almost as large as their partners. The asteroid belt also contains <a href="/wiki/Main-belt_comet" title="Main-belt comet">main-belt comets</a>, which may have been the source of Earth's water.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[84]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojans</a> are located in either of Jupiter's <a href="/wiki/L5_point" class="mw-redirect" title="L5 point">L<sub>4</sub> or L<sub>5</sub> points</a> (gravitationally stable regions leading and trailing a planet in its orbit); the term "trojan" is also used for small bodies in any other planetary or satellite Lagrange point. <a href="/wiki/Hilda_family" title="Hilda family">Hilda asteroids</a> are in a 2:3 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with Jupiter; that is, they go around the Sun three times for every two Jupiter orbits.<sup id="cite_ref-Barucci_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barucci-93">[85]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>The inner Solar System also contains <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">near-Earth asteroids</a>, many of which cross the orbits of the inner planets.<sup id="cite_ref-MorbidelliAstIII_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MorbidelliAstIII-94">[86]</a></sup> Some of them are <a href="/wiki/Potentially_hazardous_object" title="Potentially hazardous object">potentially hazardous objects</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_Solar_System">Outer Solar System</span></h2>
<p>The outer region of the Solar System is home to the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a> and their large moons. The <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">centaurs</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Short-period_comet" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-period comet">short-period comets</a> also orbit in this region. Due to their greater distance from the Sun, the solid objects in the outer Solar System contain a higher proportion of volatiles, such as water, ammonia, and methane than those of the inner Solar System because the lower temperatures allow these compounds to remain solid.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_planets">Outer planets</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Outer_planets" title="Outer planets">Outer planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">Giant planet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg/220px-Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg/330px-Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg/440px-Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2075" data-file-height="2674" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From top to bottom: <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (Montage with approximate colour and relative size)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The four outer planets, or giant planets (sometimes called Jovian planets), collectively make up 99% of the mass known to orbit the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-footnoteD_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteD-23">[g]</a></sup> Jupiter and Saturn are together over 400 times the mass of Earth and consist overwhelmingly of hydrogen and helium; Uranus and Neptune are far less massive (&lt;20 Earth masses each) and are composed primarily of ices. For these reasons, some astronomers suggest they belong in their own category, "ice giants".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup> All four giant planets have <a href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetary ring">rings</a>, although only Saturn's ring system is easily observed from Earth. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Superior_planet" class="mw-redirect" title="Superior planet">superior planet</a></i> designates planets outside Earth's orbit and thus includes both the outer planets and Mars.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter">Jupiter</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (5.2&#160;AU), at 318 Earth masses, is 2.5 times the mass of all the other planets put together. It is composed largely of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. Jupiter's strong internal heat creates semi-permanent features in its atmosphere, such as cloud bands and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a>. Jupiter has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">67 known satellites</a>. The four largest, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, show similarities to the terrestrial planets, such as volcanism and internal heating.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[88]</a></sup> Ganymede, the largest satellite in the Solar System, is larger than Mercury.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn">Saturn</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> (9.5&#160;AU), distinguished by its extensive <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">ring system</a>, has several similarities to Jupiter, such as its atmospheric composition and magnetosphere. Although Saturn has 60% of Jupiter's volume, it is less than a third as massive, at 95 Earth masses. Saturn is the only planet of the Solar System that is less dense than water.<sup id="cite_ref-preserve_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-preserve-97">[89]</a></sup> The rings of Saturn are made up of small ice and rock particles. Saturn has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">62 confirmed satellites</a> composed largely of ice. Two of these, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, show signs of geological activity.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup> Titan, the second-largest moon in the Solar System, is larger than Mercury and the only satellite in the Solar System with a substantial atmosphere.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uranus">Uranus</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> (19.2&#160;AU), at 14 Earth masses, is the lightest of the outer planets. Uniquely among the planets, it orbits the Sun on its side; its <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> is over ninety degrees to the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. It has a much colder core than the other giant planets and radiates very little heat into space.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[91]</a></sup> Uranus has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">27 known satellites</a>, the largest ones being <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neptune">Neptune</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> (30.1 AU), though slightly smaller than Uranus, is more massive (equivalent to 17 Earths) and hence more <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">dense</a>. It radiates more internal heat, but not as much as Jupiter or Saturn.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup> Neptune has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Neptune" title="Moons of Neptune">14 known satellites</a>. The largest, <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, is geologically active, with <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup> Triton is the only large satellite with a <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" class="mw-redirect" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbit</a>. Neptune is accompanied in its orbit by several <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a>, termed <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">Neptune trojans</a>, that are in 1:1 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with it.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centaurs">Centaurs</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">Centaur (minor planet)</a></div>
<p>The centaurs are icy comet-like bodies whose orbits have semi-major axes greater than Jupiter's (5.5&#160;AU) and less than Neptune's (30&#160;AU). The largest known centaur, <a href="/wiki/10199_Chariklo" title="10199 Chariklo">10199 Chariklo</a>, has a diameter of about 250&#160;km.<sup id="cite_ref-spitzer_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-102">[94]</a></sup> The first centaur discovered, <a href="/wiki/2060_Chiron" title="2060 Chiron">2060 Chiron</a>, has also been classified as comet (95P) because it develops a coma just as comets do when they approach the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comets">Comets</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg/170px-Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg/255px-Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg/340px-Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2080" data-file-height="2712" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comet-Hale-Bopp-29-03-1997_hires_adj.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" class="mw-redirect" title="Hale–Bopp">Hale–Bopp</a> seen in 1997</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">Comet</a></div>
<p>Comets are small Solar System bodies,<sup id="cite_ref-footnoteB_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-14">[e]</a></sup> typically only a few kilometres across, composed largely of volatile ices. They have highly eccentric orbits, generally a perihelion within the orbits of the inner planets and an aphelion far beyond Pluto. When a comet enters the inner Solar System, its proximity to the Sun causes its icy surface to <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">sublimate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionise</a>, creating a <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a>: a long tail of gas and dust often visible to the naked eye.</p>
<p>Short-period comets have orbits lasting less than two hundred years. Long-period comets have orbits lasting thousands of years. Short-period comets are thought to originate in the Kuiper belt, whereas long-period comets, such as <a href="/wiki/Comet_Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" title="Comet Hale–Bopp">Hale–Bopp</a>, are thought to originate in the <a href="#Oort_cloud">Oort cloud</a>. Many comet groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kreutz_Sungrazers" class="mw-redirect" title="Kreutz Sungrazers">Kreutz Sungrazers</a>, formed from the breakup of a single parent.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup> Some comets with <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_trajectory" title="Hyperbolic trajectory">hyperbolic</a> orbits may originate outside the Solar System, but determining their precise orbits is difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-hyperbolic_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hyperbolic-105">[97]</a></sup> Old comets that have had most of their volatiles driven out by solar warming are often categorised as asteroids.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trans-Neptunian_region">Trans-Neptunian region</span></h2>
<p>Beyond the orbit of Neptune lies the area of the "<a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian region</a>", with the doughnut-shaped Kuiper belt, home of Pluto and several other dwarf planets, and an overlapping disc of scattered objects, which is <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">tilted toward the plane</a> of the Solar System and reaches much further out than the Kuiper belt. The entire region is still <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Solar_System_exploration" title="Timeline of Solar System exploration">largely unexplored</a>. It appears to consist overwhelmingly of many thousands of small worlds—the largest having a diameter only a fifth that of Earth and a mass far smaller than that of the Moon—composed mainly of rock and ice. This region is sometimes described as the "third zone of the Solar System", enclosing the inner and the outer Solar System.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kuiper_belt">Kuiper belt</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png/220px-Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png/330px-Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png/440px-Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_plot_objects_of_outer_solar_system.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Known objects in the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>

<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #777777; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#aaaaaa; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojans</a></span><br />
